class="external text" href="https://designatedsites.naturalengland.org.uk/SiteDetail.aspx?SiteCode=S1001017">Natural England website</a></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The <b>Malvern Hills</b> are a range of hills in the English counties of <a href="/wiki/Worcestershire" title="Worcestershire">Worcestershire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Herefordshire" title="Herefordshire">Herefordshire</a> and a small area of northern <a href="/wiki/Gloucestershire" title="Gloucestershire">Gloucestershire</a>, dominating the surrounding countryside and the towns and villages of the district of <a href="/wiki/Malvern,_Worcestershire" title="Malvern, Worcestershire">Malvern</a>. The highest summit of the hills affords a panorama of the <a href="/wiki/Severn_Valley_(England)" class="mw-redirect" title="Severn Valley (England)">Severn valley</a> with the hills of Herefordshire and the Welsh mountains, parts of thirteen counties, the <a href="/wiki/Bristol_Channel" title="Bristol Channel">Bristol Channel</a>, and the cathedrals of Worcester, Gloucester and Hereford.\n</p><p>They are known for their <a href="/wiki/Spring_water" class="mw-redirect" title="Spring water">spring water</a> \xe2\x80\x93 initially made famous by the region\'s many <a href="/wiki/Holy_wells" class="mw-redirect" title="Holy wells">holy wells</a>, and later through the development of the 19th-century <a href="/wiki/Spa_town" title="Spa town">spa town</a> of <a href="/wiki/Great_Malvern" title="Great Malvern">Great Malvern</a>, a process which culminated in the production of the modern bottled drinking water.<sup id="cite_ref-EnglishHeritage_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EnglishHeritage-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Malvern Hills have been designated as a <a href="/wiki/Site_of_Special_Scientific_Interest" title="Site of Special Scientific Interest">Biological and Geological Site of Special Scientific Interest</a> and as <a href="/wiki/National_Character_Area" title="National Character Area">National Character Area</a> 103 by <a href="/wiki/Natural_England" title="Natural England">Natural England</a><sup id="cite_ref-SSSI_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SSSI-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NE_link_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NE_link-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> and an <a href="/wiki/Area_of_Outstanding_Natural_Beauty" title="Area of Outstanding Natural Beauty">Area of Outstanding Natural Beauty</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Countryside_Agency" title="Countryside Agency">Countryside Agency</a> (now Natural England).<sup id="cite_ref-NE_AONB_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NE_AONB-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The SSSI notification has 26 units of assessment which cover grassland, woodland and geological sites.<sup id="cite_ref-NE_units_of_assessment_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NE_units_of_assessment-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The site (The Malvern Hills SSSI (Chase End Hill)) is listed in the \'Forest of Dean Local Plan Review\' as a Key Wildlife Site (KWS).<sup id="cite_ref-FdeanLocalPlan_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FdeanLocalPlan-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Management of the hills is the responsibility of the <a href="/wiki/Malvern_Hills_Trust" class="mw-redirect" title="Malvern Hills Trust">Malvern Hills Trust</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-MalvernHillsConservators_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MalvernHillsConservators-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Simon_Jenkins" title="Simon Jenkins">Simon Jenkins</a> rates the view of the <a href="/wiki/Wye_Valley" title="Wye Valley">Wye Valley</a> and across the <a href="/wiki/Severn_Vale" class="mw-redirect" title="Severn Vale">Severn Vale</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Cotswolds" title="Cotswolds">Cotswolds</a> as one of the top ten in England.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Toponymy"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">Toponymy</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-2"><a href="#Geography"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">Geography</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-3"><a href="#Geology"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Geology</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-4"><a href="#Malvern_water"><span class="tocnumber">3.1</span> <span class="toctext">Malvern water</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-5"><a href="#Ecology"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Ecology</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-6"><a href="#History"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">History</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-7"><a href="#Governance"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Governance</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-8"><a href="#Sport,_leisure,_and_tourism"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Sport, leisure, and tourism</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-9"><a href="#Abberley_and_Malvern_Hills_Geopark"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Abberley and Malvern Hills Geopark</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-10"><a href="#Transport"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Transport</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-11"><a href="#Malvern_Hills_in_cultural_life"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Malvern Hills in cultural life</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-12"><a href="#Music"><span class="tocnumber">10.1</span> <span class="toctext">Music</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-13"><a href="#Literature"><span class="tocnumber">10.2</span> <span class="toctext">Literature</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-14"><a href="#Art"><span class="tocnumber">10.3</span> <span class="toctext">Art</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-15"><a href="#Television"><span class="tocnumber">10.4</span> <span class="toctext">Television</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-16"><a href="#The_Hills"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">The Hills</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-17"><a href="#Vistas"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">Vistas</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-18"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-20"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">15</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Toponymy">Toponymy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Malvern_Hills&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Toponymy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The name Malvern is probably derived from the <a href="/wiki/British_language_(Celtic)" class="mw-redirect" title="British language (Celtic)">ancient British</a> <i>moel-bryn</i>, meaning "Bare-Hill",<sup id="cite_ref-Smart2009_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smart2009-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> the nearest modern equivalent being the <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">Welsh</a> <i>moelfryn</i> (bald hill).<sup id="cite_ref-Walters1828_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Walters1828-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> It has been known as Malferna (11th century), Malverne (12th century), and Much Malvern (16th and 17th centuries).<sup id="cite_ref-Page&amp;Willis-Bund1924_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Page&amp;Willis-Bund1924-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Jabez_Allies" title="Jabez Allies">Jabez Allies</a>, a 19th-century <a href="/wiki/Antiquarian" title="Antiquarian">antiquarian</a> from Worcestershire speculated that \'vern\' was derived from the British words \'Sarn\' or \'Varn\' meaning pavement or seat of judgement.<sup id="cite_ref-Allies1852_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Allies1852-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Malvern_Hills&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The Malvern Hills are part of an Area of Outstanding Natural Beauty, with scenic views over both Herefordshire and Worcestershire. The Hills run north/south for about 13&#160;km (8 miles), in between <a href="/wiki/Great_Malvern" title="Great Malvern">Great Malvern</a> and the village of <a href="/wiki/Colwall" title="Colwall">Colwall</a>, and overlook the <a href="/wiki/River_Severn" title="River Severn">River Severn</a> valley to the east, with the <a href="/wiki/Cotswolds" title="Cotswolds">Cotswolds</a> beyond. The highest point of the hills is the <a href="/wiki/Worcestershire_Beacon" title="Worcestershire Beacon">Worcestershire Beacon</a> at 425 metres (1,394&#160;ft) above sea level (OS Grid reference SO768452). The hills are famous for their natural mineral springs and wells, which were responsible for the development of <a href="/wiki/Great_Malvern" title="Great Malvern">Great Malvern</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Spa_town" title="Spa town">spa</a> in the early 19th century. Until recently, <a href="/wiki/Malvern_water" title="Malvern water">Malvern water</a> was bottled commercially on a large scale and sold worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-CocaColaPullsThePlug_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CocaColaPullsThePlug-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>There are three passes over the hills, the Wyche cutting, the <a href="/wiki/A438_road" class="mw-redirect" title="A438 road">A438 road</a> north of <a href="/wiki/Raggedstone_Hill" title="Raggedstone Hill">Raggedstone Hill</a> and the <a href="/wiki/A449_road" title="A449 road">A449 road</a> just north of the <a href="/wiki/Herefordshire_Beacon" title="Herefordshire Beacon">Herefordshire Beacon</a>, the site of the <a href="/wiki/British_Camp" title="British Camp">British Camp</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a> <a href="/wiki/Hill_fort" class="mw-redirect" title="Hill fort">hill fort</a> at the top of the hill. The site is thought to date back before the <a href="/wiki/Common_Era" title="Common Era">Common Era</a> and has been extended subsequently by a medieval <a href="/wiki/Castle" title="Castle">castle</a>. The extensive <a href="/wiki/Earthworks_(archaeology)" title="Earthworks (archaeology)">earthworks</a> remain clearly visible today and determine the shape of the hill.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geology">Geology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Malvern_Hills&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Geology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gullet_Quarry_-_from_East_-_geograph.org.uk_-_5651.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Gullet_Quarry_-_from_East_-_geograph.org.uk_-_5651.jpg/220px-Gullet_Quarry_-_from_East_-_geograph.org.uk_-_5651.jpg" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Gullet_Quarry_-_from_East_-_geograph.org.uk_-_5651.jpg/330px-Gullet_Quarry_-_from_East_-_geograph.org.uk_-_5651.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Gullet_Quarry_-_from_East_-_geograph.org.uk_-_5651.jpg/440px-Gullet_Quarry_-_from_East_-_geograph.org.uk_-_5651.jpg 2x" data-file-width="480" data-file-height="640" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gullet_Quarry_-_from_East_-_geograph.org.uk_-_5651.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gullet Quarry and <a href="/wiki/Unconformity" title="Unconformity">unconformity</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The Malvern Hills are formed of some of the most ancient rocks in England, mostly <a href="/wiki/Igneous" class="mw-redirect" title="Igneous">igneous</a> and <a href="/wiki/Metamorphic_rock" title="Metamorphic rock">metamorphic rocks</a> from the late <a href="/wiki/Precambrian" title="Precambrian">Precambrian</a>, known as the <a href="/wiki/Uriconian" title="Uriconian">Uriconian</a>, which are around 680 million years old.<sup id="cite_ref-AbberleyGeopark_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AbberleyGeopark-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MalvernsComplex_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MalvernsComplex-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>\nThe <a href="/wiki/Malvern_Line" title="Malvern Line">Malvern Line</a> or Malvern Lineament is the name applied to a north-south aligned <a href="/wiki/Lineament" title="Lineament">lineament</a> which runs through the Malvern Hills and extends southwards towards <a href="/wiki/Bristol" title="Bristol">Bristol</a> and northwards past <a href="/wiki/Kidderminster" title="Kidderminster">Kidderminster</a>. It consists of a series of <a href="/wiki/Fault_(geology)" title="Fault (geology)">faults</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fold_(geology)" title="Fold (geology)">folds</a> which have the effect of bringing old <a href="/w/index.php?title=Malvernian&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Malvernian (page does not exist)">Malvernian</a> rocks to the surface. Being largely hard <a href="/wiki/Igneous" class="mw-redirect" title="Igneous">igneous</a> rocks, they have resisted <a href="/wiki/Erosion" title="Erosion">erosion</a> better than those of the surrounding countryside and result in a striking line of hills of which the Malvern Hills are the most impressive. This line is considered to mark the edge of two <a href="/wiki/Terrane" title="Terrane">terranes</a> \xe2\x80\x93 two once separate fragments of the Earth\'s crust now joined as one \xe2\x80\x93 the <a href="/wiki/Wrekin_Terrane" title="Wrekin Terrane">Wrekin Terrane</a> to the west and the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Charnwood_Terrane&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Charnwood Terrane (page does not exist)">Charnwood Terrane</a> to the east.\n</p><p>The main face of Gullet Quarry shows a cross-section through the Precambrian rock and exhibits many rock types including <a href="/wiki/Diorite" title="Diorite">diorite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Granite" title="Granite">granite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gneiss" title="Gneiss">gneiss</a>, <a href="/wiki/Schist" title="Schist">schist</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pegmatite" title="Pegmatite">pegmatite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dolerite" class="mw-redirect" title="Dolerite">dolerite</a>. The evidence of the complex history of earth movements which formed the Hills can be seen by multiple joints, fractures, faults and shears, which make identifying changes in rock types difficult. Mineral deposits such as <a href="/wiki/Haematite" class="mw-redirect" title="Haematite">haematite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Calcite" title="Calcite">calcite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Epidote" title="Epidote">epidote</a> can be seen within these features.<sup id="cite_ref-WestMidlandsGeodiversityPartnership_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WestMidlandsGeodiversityPartnership-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-GulletQuarry_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GulletQuarry-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>There is a tiny, man-made cave near the ridge of the hills called Clutter\'s Cave (or Giant\'s Cave or Hermit\'s Cave or Waum\'s Cave, after Walm\'s Well which is located on the boundary of News Wood below).<sup id="cite_ref-BBCPlaces_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBCPlaces-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WalmsWell_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WalmsWell-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> The cave has been excavated into <a href="/wiki/Pillow_lavas" class="mw-redirect" title="Pillow lavas">pillow lavas</a>. Some of the rounded \'pillow\' shapes are still visible around the entrance to the cave.<sup id="cite_ref-WestMidlandsGeodiversityPartnershipHerefordshire_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WestMidlandsGeodiversityPartnershipHerefordshire-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Malvern_water">Malvern water</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Malvern_Hills&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Malvern water">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Malvern_water" title="Malvern water">Malvern water</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Hydrogeology" title="Hydrogeology">Hydrogeology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Groundwater_recharge" title="Groundwater recharge">Groundwater recharge</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:St._Anne%27s_Well_by_Jim_Linwood.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/St._Anne%27s_Well_by_Jim_Linwood.jpg/220px-St._Anne%27s_Well_by_Jim_Linwood.jpg" width="220" height="128" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/St._Anne%27s_Well_by_Jim_Linwood.jpg/330px-St._Anne%27s_Well_by_Jim_Linwood.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/St._Anne%27s_Well_by_Jim_Linwood.jpg/440px-St._Anne%27s_Well_by_Jim_Linwood.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3996" data-file-height="2326" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:St._Anne%27s_Well_by_Jim_Linwood.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/St._Ann%27s_Well,_Malvern" title="St. Ann&#39;s Well, Malvern">St. Ann\'s Well</a>, Great Malvern, a popular caf\xc3\xa9 for walkers on the hills. The building on the right houses the spout from which the water surges into a basin.</div></div></div>\n<p>The quality of Malvern water is attributable to its source. The rocks of the Malvern Hills are amongst the oldest and hardest found in England; the geology is responsible for the quality of Malvern\'s spring water.<sup id="cite_ref-Smart2009_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smart2009-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-LaMoreaux&amp;Tanner2001_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LaMoreaux&amp;Tanner2001-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> The hills consist of <a href="/wiki/Precambrian" title="Precambrian">Precambrian</a> <a href="/wiki/Igneous" class="mw-redirect" title="Igneous">igneous</a> and <a href="/wiki/Metamorphic_rock" title="Metamorphic rock">metamorphic rock</a>, the oldest of which are about 670 million years old.<sup id="cite_ref-GulletQuarry_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GulletQuarry-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gibbons-Harris1994_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gibbons-Harris1994-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> The rocks are characterised by low porosity and high secondary permeability via fissures.<sup id="cite_ref-Hydrology&amp;Geomorphology_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hydrology&amp;Geomorphology-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HydrogeologyMalvernHills_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HydrogeologyMalvernHills-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Malvern water is rainwater and snow meltwater that percolates through fissures created by the pressures of <a href="/wiki/Tectonic" class="mw-redirect" title="Tectonic">tectonic</a> movements about 300 million years ago when advancing sedimentary layers of <a href="/wiki/Silurian" title="Silurian">Silurian</a> <a href="/wiki/Shale" title="Shale">shale</a> and <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone</a> were pushed into and under older Precambrian rock.<sup id="cite_ref-LaMoreaux&amp;Tanner2001_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LaMoreaux&amp;Tanner2001-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HydrogeologyMalvernHills_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HydrogeologyMalvernHills-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-geo_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-geo-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> When the fissures are saturated, a <a href="/wiki/Water_table" title="Water table">water table</a> forms and the water emerges as springs around the fault lines between the strata. Depending on rainfall, the flow can vary from as little as 36 litres (7.9&#160;imp&#160;gal; 9.5&#160;US&#160;gal) per minute to over 350 litres (77&#160;imp&#160;gal; 92&#160;US&#160;gal) per minute.<sup id="cite_ref-Blyth1967_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blyth1967-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> The water permeates through the rock which, because of its hardness, leaves little or no mineral traces in the water, while at the same time the very fine cracks act as a filter for other impurities.<sup id="cite_ref-HydrogeologyMalvernHills_24-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HydrogeologyMalvernHills-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Rainfall on the Malvern Hills is thought to be sufficient to account for all the water that runs out of the springs, reflected for example in some spring flows six to eight weeks after heavy rainfall, and in reduced flows after a dry period.<sup id="cite_ref-CollectingMalvernWater_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CollectingMalvernWater-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecology">Ecology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Malvern_Hills&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Ecology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The Malvern Hills have been designated as a Site of Special Scientific Interest (SSSI) by Natural England<sup id="cite_ref-SSSI_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SSSI-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NE_link_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NE_link-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> and an Area of Outstanding Natural Beauty by the Countryside Agency (now Natural England).<sup id="cite_ref-NE_AONB_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NE_AONB-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Features of the Malvern Hills AONB include wide areas of acid grassland and <a href="/wiki/Heath" title="Heath">heath</a> on the summit and <a href="/wiki/Mixed_woodland" class="mw-redirect" title="Mixed woodland">mixed broadleaved woodland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_woodland" title="Ancient woodland">Ancient Semi-Natural Woodland</a> on the lower hills and valleys.<sup id="cite_ref-MHAONBLandscape_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MHAONBLandscape-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> There are three areas of Ancient Semi-Natural Woodland in the Malvern Hills SSSI: <a href="/wiki/Hollybush,_Worcestershire" title="Hollybush, Worcestershire">Hollybush</a> Roughs between the boundary of <a href="/wiki/Castlemorton" title="Castlemorton">Castlemorton Common</a> and the Midsummer Hill fort, Park Wood in <a href="/wiki/West_Malvern" title="West Malvern">West Malvern</a> and an area near <a href="/wiki/Holy_Well,_Malvern" title="Holy Well, Malvern">Holy Well</a> above <a href="/wiki/Malvern_Wells" title="Malvern Wells">Malvern Wells</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-MHCManagementPlan_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MHCManagementPlan-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Key AONB species include <a href="/wiki/Dormouse" title="Dormouse">dormouse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barbastelle" title="Barbastelle">barbastelle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Skylark" class="mw-redirect" title="Skylark">skylark</a>, <a href="/wiki/High_brown_fritillary_butterfly" class="mw-redirect" title="High brown fritillary butterfly">high brown fritillary butterfly</a>, <a href="/wiki/Great_crested_newt" class="mw-redirect" title="Great crested newt">great crested newt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vipera_berus" title="Vipera berus">adder</a> and <a href="/wiki/Black_poplar" class="mw-redirect" title="Black poplar">black poplar</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-MHAONBBiodiversity_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MHAONBBiodiversity-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Malvern_Hills&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:British_camp_central_mound_2005.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/British_camp_central_mound_2005.jpg/220px-British_camp_central_mound_2005.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/British_camp_central_mound_2005.jpg/330px-British_camp_central_mound_2005.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/British_camp_central_mound_2005.jpg/440px-British_camp_central_mound_2005.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2272" data-file-height="1704" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:British_camp_central_mound_2005.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Iron Age earthworks, <a href="/wiki/British_camp" class="mw-redirect" title="British camp">British camp</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Flint axes, arrowheads, and flakes found in the area are attributed to early <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> settlers,<sup id="cite_ref-Smith1978_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith1978-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> and the \'<a href="/wiki/British_Camp#The_Shire_Ditch" title="British Camp">Shire Ditch</a>\', a late Bronze Age boundary earthwork possibly dating from around 1000&#160;BC, was constructed along part of the crest of the hills near the site of later settlements.<sup id="cite_ref-EnglishHeritage_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EnglishHeritage-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> The Wyche Cutting, a mountain pass through the hills was in use in prehistoric times as part of the salt route from <a href="/wiki/Droitwich_Spa" title="Droitwich Spa">Droitwich</a> to South Wales.<sup id="cite_ref-Smith1978_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith1978-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> A 19th-century discovery of over two hundred metal money bars suggests that the area had been inhabited by the <a href="/wiki/La_T%C3%A8ne_culture" title="La T\xc3\xa8ne culture">La T\xc3\xa8ne people</a> around 250&#160;BC.<sup id="cite_ref-Smith1978_31-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith1978-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Ancient folklore has it that the <a href="/wiki/British_Iron_Age" title="British Iron Age">British</a> chieftain <a href="/wiki/Caractacus" class="mw-redirect" title="Caractacus">Caractacus</a> made his last stand against the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Romans</a> at the <a href="/wiki/British_Camp" title="British Camp">British Camp</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-BBCJune2003_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBCJune2003-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> a site of extensive Iron Age earthworks on a summit of the Malvern Hills close to where Malvern was to be later established. The story remains disputed, however, as Roman historian <a href="/wiki/Tacitus" title="Tacitus">Tacitus</a> implies a site closer to the river Severn.<sup id="cite_ref-Woodman2004_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Woodman2004-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> There is therefore no evidence that Roman presence ended the prehistoric settlement at British Camp. However, excavations at nearby <a href="/wiki/Midsummer_Hill" title="Midsummer Hill">Midsummer Hillfort</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bredon_Hill" title="Bredon Hill">Bredon Hill</a> and <a href="/wiki/Croft_Ambrey" title="Croft Ambrey">Croft Ambrey</a> all show evidence of violent destruction around the year 48&#160;AD. This may suggest that the British Camp was abandoned or destroyed around the same time.<sup id="cite_ref-Hencken1938_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hencken1938-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:View_N_from_western_peak_of_Ragged_Stone_Hill_-_geograph.org.uk_-_35228.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/View_N_from_western_peak_of_Ragged_Stone_Hill_-_geograph.org.uk_-_35228.jpg/220px-View_N_from_western_peak_of_Ragged_Stone_Hill_-_geograph.org.uk_-_35228.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/View_N_from_western_peak_of_Ragged_Stone_Hill_-_geograph.org.uk_-_35228.jpg/330px-View_N_from_western_peak_of_Ragged_Stone_Hill_-_geograph.org.uk_-_35228.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/View_N_from_western_peak_of_Ragged_Stone_Hill_-_geograph.org.uk_-_35228.jpg/440px-View_N_from_western_peak_of_Ragged_Stone_Hill_-_geograph.org.uk_-_35228.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="479" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:View_N_from_western_peak_of_Ragged_Stone_Hill_-_geograph.org.uk_-_35228.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Midsummer_Hill" title="Midsummer Hill">Midsummer Hill Iron Age Hill Fort</a> and Hollybush Quarry</div></div></div>\n<p>During the medieval period, the hills and surrounding area were part of a <a href="/wiki/Royal_forest" title="Royal forest">Royal forest</a> known as <a href="/wiki/Malvern_Chase" title="Malvern Chase">Malvern Chase</a>. Riots by commoners and legal challenges from land owners ensued when <a href="/wiki/Charles_I_of_England" title="Charles I of England">King Charles I</a> attempted to disafforest the Chase in 1630. Ultimately, only one third was disafforested, and commissioners were appointed to ensure any further encroachments did not leave the common lands as the most meagre in quality. This system lasted until the 1800s.<sup id="cite_ref-Lees_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lees-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1884, the <a href="/wiki/Malvern_Hills_Conservators" title="Malvern Hills Conservators">Malvern Hills Conservators</a> were established through an Act of <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Parliament of the United Kingdom">Parliament</a> to preserve the natural aspect of the hills and protect them from encroachments.<sup id="cite_ref-MalvernHillsConservatorsBBCHW_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MalvernHillsConservatorsBBCHW-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> However, by this time large-scale quarrying had already begun. Quarry works were set in motion in the 1870s at Tank Quarry and at Little Malvern by Pyx Granite Company. The Hills Conservators lobbied parliament to pass an Act limiting the exploitation, and although a second Act was passed in 1924 its provisions were largely ineffective. Quarrying continued until 1966.<sup id="cite_ref-QuarriesInTheMalvernHills_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-QuarriesInTheMalvernHills-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> The landscape itself was irrevocably changed;<sup id="cite_ref-TankQuarry_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TankQuarry-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> but there is some debate whether this has enriched or damaged the ecology of the Hills.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2008)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Certainly the quarrying has changed the Hills forever, including creating habitats for frogs, toads, newts and other small animals. The new cliffs provide nesting sites for certain birds.<sup id="cite_ref-MalvernHillsConservatorsBirdsandBotany_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MalvernHillsConservatorsBirdsandBotany-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> The quarries, especially North Quarry and Tank Quarry, have over the years been the sites of several accidents requiring the Emergency Services.<sup id="cite_ref-DiversRecoverBody_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DiversRecoverBody-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-YouthsrescuedfromMalvernquarry_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-YouthsrescuedfromMalvernquarry-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rescuerloweredtoquarryquartet_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rescuerloweredtoquarryquartet-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cattle_on_the_Malvern_Hills_-_geograph.org.uk_-_371551.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Cattle_on_the_Malvern_Hills_-_geograph.org.uk_-_371551.jpg/220px-Cattle_on_the_Malvern_Hills_-_geograph.org.uk_-_371551.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Cattle_on_the_Malvern_Hills_-_geograph.org.uk_-_371551.jpg/330px-Cattle_on_the_Malvern_Hills_-_geograph.org.uk_-_371551.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Cattle_on_the_Malvern_Hills_-_geograph.org.uk_-_371551.jpg/440px-Cattle_on_the_Malvern_Hills_-_geograph.org.uk_-_371551.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="426" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cattle_on_the_Malvern_Hills_-_geograph.org.uk_-_371551.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Dexter_cattle" title="Dexter cattle">Dexter cattle</a> on the Malvern Hills</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1989, the cafe on Worcestershire Beacon burned down. As the Malvern Hills Acts state that no building should be erected on the Conservators\' land or on land under their jurisdiction, the Conservators put a bill through Parliament to get the power to build a new one but the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">House of Lords</a> opposed it.<sup id="cite_ref-Fire_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fire-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> When the cafe was burned down, the Conservators had plans to replace the building but were advised that they risked prosecution for rebuilding as the original cafe building was an encroachment on common land. The Malvern Hills Bill was in preparation to modernise some of the clauses in previous acts a clause was inserted to gain authority to rebuild the cafe.\nFive members of the House of Lords <a href="/wiki/Select_committee_(Westminster_system)" class="mw-redirect" title="Select committee (Westminster system)">Select Committee</a> visited the Malvern Hills and decided that there were enough facilities in the immediate area and that St Ann\'s Well cafe should be enough provision on the hills, so the application to rebuild was turned down.<sup id="cite_ref-ImpossibleIdea_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ImpossibleIdea-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MalvernHillsBill1993_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MalvernHillsBill1993-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MalvernHillsBill1994_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MalvernHillsBill1994-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Holy_Well,_Malvern_Wells_-_geograph.org.uk_-_395887.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/The_Holy_Well%2C_Malvern_Wells_-_geograph.org.uk_-_395887.jpg/220px-The_Holy_Well%2C_Malvern_Wells_-_geograph.org.uk_-_395887.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/The_Holy_Well%2C_Malvern_Wells_-_geograph.org.uk_-_395887.jpg/330px-The_Holy_Well%2C_Malvern_Wells_-_geograph.org.uk_-_395887.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/The_Holy_Well%2C_Malvern_Wells_-_geograph.org.uk_-_395887.jpg/440px-The_Holy_Well%2C_Malvern_Wells_-_geograph.org.uk_-_395887.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="427" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Holy_Well,_Malvern_Wells_-_geograph.org.uk_-_395887.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Holy_Well,_Malvern" title="Holy Well, Malvern">The Holy Well</a>, Malvern. With the aid of a Lottery Heritage grant, production of 1200 bottles per day of Holy Well Spring Water was recommenced.</div></div></div>\n<p>In 2000, a \xc2\xa31.3 million project to reintroduce grazing animals to the Malvern Hills and restore part of its historic network of water spouts was given significant backing of <a href="/wiki/National_Lottery_(United_Kingdom)" title="National Lottery (United Kingdom)">National Lottery</a> funds. The Malverns Heritage Project aimed to reintroduce grazing animals as a natural means of scrub management and restore several water features. The project was spearheaded by the Malvern Hills AONB Service, in partnership with Worcestershire County Council, Herefordshire Council, Malvern Hills Conservators, Malvern Spa Association, <a href="/wiki/English_Nature" title="English Nature">English Nature</a>, Countryside Agency, <a href="/wiki/National_Trust_for_Places_of_Historic_Interest_or_Natural_Beauty" title="National Trust for Places of Historic Interest or Natural Beauty">National Trust</a> and <a href="/wiki/English_Heritage" title="English Heritage">English Heritage</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ProjectToSaveHills_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ProjectToSaveHills-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Members of the public were concerned that by erecting temporary fences on the Malvern Hills the Conservators would be straying from their core duty of keeping the Malvern Hills unenclosed as open spaces for the recreation and enjoyment of the public.  Although the conservation officer said any enclosures would be small and temporary there were worries that leisure activities that could be affected and that "the feeling of freedom associated with \'just being\' on the Malvern Hills" could be lost.<sup id="cite_ref-StrayingFromDuty_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StrayingFromDuty-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-DontFence_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DontFence-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-OurFreedom_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OurFreedom-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2001, the Malvern Hills were officially closed to the public for the first time in history. Walkers were told to avoid the area as part of the effort to prevent the spread of <a href="/wiki/Foot_and_mouth_disease" class="mw-redirect" title="Foot and mouth disease">foot and mouth disease</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-KeepOff_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KeepOff-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> As a result of the closure the economy of the Malvern Hills faced serious damage.<sup id="cite_ref-ItCantGoOn_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ItCantGoOn-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> In 2002 the Malvern Hills were named the most popular free tourist attraction in the West Midlands in a survey commissioned by the Countryside Agency to take the temperature of rural tourism in the wake of the crisis.<sup id="cite_ref-HillsAreTop_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HillsAreTop-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2006, Worcestershire County Council was awarded \xc2\xa3770,000 by the Heritage Lottery Fund for restoration work and preservation of the area by fitting cattle grids to roads across the Hills and encouraging local landowners to allow sheep to wander across their land.  As part of the Malvern Heritage Project nine water features within the AONB were also renovated.<sup id="cite_ref-MalvernHeritageProject_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MalvernHeritageProject-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Governance">Governance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Malvern_Hills&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Governance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Malvern_Hills_AONB.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Malvern_Hills_AONB.jpg/220px-Malvern_Hills_AONB.jpg" width="220" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Malvern_Hills_AONB.jpg/330px-Malvern_Hills_AONB.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Malvern_Hills_AONB.jpg/440px-Malvern_Hills_AONB.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3965" data-file-height="2832" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Malvern_Hills_AONB.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Malvern Hills with the <a href="/wiki/British_Camp" title="British Camp">British Camp</a> on the left</div></div></div>\n<p>The Malvern Hills Conservators manage most parts of the Hills and the surrounding Commons, some other parcels of land and many roadside verges. They were established in 1884 and are governed by five <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Parliament_in_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Acts of Parliament in the United Kingdom">Acts of Parliament</a>, the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Malvern_Hills_Acts&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Malvern Hills Acts (page does not exist)">Malvern Hills Acts</a> 1884, 1909, 1924, 1930 and 1995. They are a voluntary body of twenty-nine members. Eleven are directly elected under the Local Elections (<a href="/wiki/Principal_areas" class="mw-redirect" title="Principal areas">Principal areas</a>) Rules by the residents of the <a href="/wiki/Ward_(country_subdivision)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ward (country subdivision)">wards</a> who contribute to the Conservators\' funds through a <a href="/wiki/Tax" title="Tax">levy</a> in their <a href="/wiki/Council_Tax" title="Council Tax">Council Tax</a>, seventeen are appointed by <a href="/wiki/Local_authorities" class="mw-redirect" title="Local authorities">local authorities</a> and one by the <a href="/wiki/Church_Commissioners" title="Church Commissioners">Church Commissioners</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-MalvernHillsConservators_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MalvernHillsConservators-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> The total area under their jurisdiction is over 1,200 hectares (3,000 acres).<sup id="cite_ref-MalvernHillsConservatorsAbout_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MalvernHillsConservatorsAbout-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Malvern Hills were designated an Area of Outstanding Natural Beauty (AONB) in 1959. The designation covers 105 square kilometres (41&#160;sq&#160;mi) and includes parts of Herefordshire, Worcestershire and Gloucestershire.<sup id="cite_ref-MalvernHillsAONB_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MalvernHillsAONB-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> The Malvern Hills Conservators played a key role in ensuring that the area of the AONB is larger than that originally proposed.<sup id="cite_ref-MalvernHillsAONBHistory_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MalvernHillsAONBHistory-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The AONB Partnership work together to conserve and enhance the Malvern Hills AONB. The Partnership has a formal structure including representatives from private and public enterprises, officers from local authorities, the Countryside Agency and the Malvern Hills Conservators.<sup id="cite_ref-MalvernHillsAONBStructure_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MalvernHillsAONBStructure-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Sport.2C_leisure.2C_and_tourism"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Sport,_leisure,_and_tourism">Sport, leisure, and tourism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Malvern_Hills&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Sport, leisure, and tourism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Walkers_on_the_Malvern_Hills_-_geograph.org.uk_-_825312.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Walkers_on_the_Malvern_Hills_-_geograph.org.uk_-_825312.jpg/220px-Walkers_on_the_Malvern_Hills_-_geograph.org.uk_-_825312.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Walkers_on_the_Malvern_Hills_-_geograph.org.uk_-_825312.jpg/330px-Walkers_on_the_Malvern_Hills_-_geograph.org.uk_-_825312.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Walkers_on_the_Malvern_Hills_-_geograph.org.uk_-_825312.jpg/440px-Walkers_on_the_Malvern_Hills_-_geograph.org.uk_-_825312.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Walkers_on_the_Malvern_Hills_-_geograph.org.uk_-_825312.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Walkers on the Malvern Hills</div></div></div>\n<p>The Malvern Hills are home to a wide range of outdoor sports and leisure activities, including walking, mountain biking, horse riding, orienteering, hang-gliding, paragliding, model aircraft flying, fishing, climbing and diving.<sup id="cite_ref-MalvernHillsConservatorsActivities_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MalvernHillsConservatorsActivities-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Worcestershire_Way" title="Worcestershire Way">Worcestershire Way</a> is a waymarked <a href="/wiki/Long-distance_trail" title="Long-distance trail">long-distance trail</a> located within the county of <a href="/wiki/Worcestershire" title="Worcestershire">Worcestershire</a>. It runs 50&#160;km (31 miles) from <a href="/wiki/Bewdley" title="Bewdley">Bewdley</a> to <a href="/wiki/Great_Malvern" title="Great Malvern">Great Malvern</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-WorcestershireWay_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WorcestershireWay-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> It is an important recreation resource in the AONB.<sup id="cite_ref-NauralEngland_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NauralEngland-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Geopark_Way" title="Geopark Way">Geopark Way</a> is a 175&#160;km (109 miles) long-distance trail which runs from <a href="/wiki/Bridgnorth" title="Bridgnorth">Bridgnorth</a> to <a href="/wiki/Gloucester" title="Gloucester">Gloucester</a> and passes through the Abberley and Malvern Hills Geopark.<sup id="cite_ref-109Mile_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109Mile-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> The route was devised to highlight geology, landscape and associated heritage.<sup id="cite_ref-GeoparkWay_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GeoparkWay-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> The Malvern Hills Geocentre is located halfway along the <a href="/wiki/Geopark_Way" title="Geopark Way">Geopark Way</a>, at the <a href="/wiki/Wyche,_Worcestershire" title="Wyche, Worcestershire">Wyche</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> This official visitor centre gives more information on interactive <a href="/wiki/IPads" class="mw-redirect" title="IPads">iPads</a> about the geology, nature and history of the Geopark and the Malvern hills and Malvern in particular, as well as large wall maps of the area.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Abberley_and_Malvern_Hills_Geopark">Abberley and Malvern Hills Geopark</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Malvern_Hills&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Abberley and Malvern Hills Geopark">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The <a href="/w/index.php?title=Abberley_and_Malvern_Hills_Geopark&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Abberley and Malvern Hills Geopark (page does not exist)">Abberley and Malvern Hills Geopark</a> was launched in 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-GeoparkLaunch_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GeoparkLaunch-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> It falls within the counties of Herefordshire, Gloucestershire, <a href="/wiki/Shropshire" title="Shropshire">Shropshire</a> and Worcestershire and covers 3,240&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (1,250 square miles). The geological and <a href="/wiki/Geomorphological" class="mw-redirect" title="Geomorphological">geomorphological</a> significance of the area has been recognised for many years with 13 <a href="/wiki/Sites_of_Special_Scientific_Interest" class="mw-redirect" title="Sites of Special Scientific Interest">Sites of Special Scientific Interest</a> (SSSI) and 179 Local Geological Sites (LGS) present.<sup id="cite_ref-GeoparkBAckground_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GeoparkBAckground-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> The Abberley and Malvern Hills Geopark is one of only seven geoparks in the UK.<sup id="cite_ref-109Mile_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109Mile-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Transport">Transport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Malvern_Hills&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Transport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/A449" class="mw-redirect" title="A449">A449 road</a> runs through the centre of Malvern, connecting it to <a href="/wiki/Worcester" title="Worcester">Worcester</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ledbury" title="Ledbury">Ledbury</a>. The <a href="/wiki/M5_motorway" title="M5 motorway">M5 motorway</a> to the east of Malvern is accessible at junctions 7 and 8. The <a href="/wiki/M50_motorway_(Great_Britain)" title="M50 motorway (Great Britain)">M50</a> (also known as the <i>Ross Spur</i>) to the south can be accessed at junction 1 on the <a href="/wiki/A38_road" title="A38 road">A38 road</a> between Tewkesbury and Malvern.\n</p><p>The AONB has four Railway stations inside or very close to its boundary \xe2\x80\x93 <a href="/wiki/Malvern_Link_railway_station" title="Malvern Link railway station">Malvern Link</a>, <a href="/wiki/Great_Malvern_railway_station" title="Great Malvern railway station">Great Malvern</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colwall_railway_station" title="Colwall railway station">Colwall</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ledbury_railway_station" title="Ledbury railway station">Ledbury</a>. These railway stations lie on the <a href="/wiki/Cotswold_Line" title="Cotswold Line">Cotswolds &amp; Malverns Line</a> which operates between <a href="/wiki/Oxford_railway_station" title="Oxford railway station">Oxford</a> via <a href="/wiki/Worcester_Shrub_Hill_railway_station" title="Worcester Shrub Hill railway station">Worcester Shrub Hill</a> and <a href="/wiki/Worcester_Foregate_Street_railway_station" title="Worcester Foregate Street railway station">Worcester Foregate Street</a> to <a href="/wiki/Hereford_railway_station" title="Hereford railway station">Hereford</a>. Direct trains to the area are available from <a href="/wiki/Birmingham_Snow_Hill_railway_station" title="Birmingham Snow Hill railway station">Birmingham Snow Hill</a> or <a href="/wiki/Birmingham_New_Street_railway_station" title="Birmingham New Street railway station">Birmingham New Street</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paddington_railway_station" class="mw-redirect" title="Paddington railway station">London Paddington</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-MalvernHillsAONBPublicTransport_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MalvernHillsAONBPublicTransport-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Several local bus services connect Malvern with the surrounding area.<sup id="cite_ref-busroutesmap_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-busroutesmap-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Long-distance direct bus services connect Malvern with other cities in the country, including the National Express route 321 through eleven counties from Aberdare in South Wales via Birmingham and other major cities, to Bradford in West Yorkshire,<sup id="cite_ref-carlberry_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-carlberry-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> and route 444 from Worcester to London (Victoria).<sup id="cite_ref-carlberry_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-carlberry-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Malvern_Hills_in_cultural_life">Malvern Hills in cultural life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Malvern_Hills&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Malvern Hills in cultural life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Malvern_Hills&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sir_Edward_Elgar,_Belle_Vue_Island,_Great_Malvern_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1316288.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Sir_Edward_Elgar%2C_Belle_Vue_Island%2C_Great_Malvern_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1316288.jpg/220px-Sir_Edward_Elgar%2C_Belle_Vue_Island%2C_Great_Malvern_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1316288.jpg" width="220" height="330" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Sir_Edward_Elgar%2C_Belle_Vue_Island%2C_Great_Malvern_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1316288.jpg/330px-Sir_Edward_Elgar%2C_Belle_Vue_Island%2C_Great_Malvern_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1316288.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/de/Sir_Edward_Elgar%2C_Belle_Vue_Island%2C_Great_Malvern_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1316288.jpg 2x" data-file-width="427" data-file-height="640" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sir_Edward_Elgar,_Belle_Vue_Island,_Great_Malvern_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1316288.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sir Edward Elgar, Belle Vue Island, Great Malvern</div></div></div>\n<p>English composer <a href="/wiki/Edward_Elgar" title="Edward Elgar">Edward Elgar</a>, who was from the area, often walked, cycled, and reportedly flew kites on these hills. He wrote a <a href="/wiki/Cantata" title="Cantata">cantata</a> in 1898 entitled <i><a href="/wiki/Caractacus" class="mw-redirect" title="Caractacus">Caractacus</a></i>, which alludes to the popular legend of his last stand at British Camp.<sup id="cite_ref-InTuneWithElgar_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-InTuneWithElgar-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Moore1999_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moore1999-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> In 1934, during the composer\'s final illness, he told a friend: "If ever after I\'m dead you hear someone whistling this tune [from his <a href="/wiki/Cello_Concerto_(Elgar)" title="Cello Concerto (Elgar)">Cello Concerto</a>] on the Malvern Hills, don\'t be alarmed, it\'s only me."<sup id="cite_ref-Kennedy1982_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kennedy1982-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Composers <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Howells" title="Herbert Howells">Herbert Howells</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ivor_Gurney" title="Ivor Gurney">Ivor Gurney</a> used to take long walks together through the nearby <a href="/wiki/Cotswold_Hills" class="mw-redirect" title="Cotswold Hills">Cotswold Hills</a> and the natural beauty of the area, including the magnificent views of the Malverns, was a profound inspiration for their music. Howells dedicated his first major work, the Piano Quartet in A minor (1916), to "the hill at Chosen (<a href="/wiki/Churchdown" title="Churchdown">Churchdown</a>) and Ivor Gurney who knew it".<sup id="cite_ref-LongRememberedHills_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LongRememberedHills-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Swedish singer <a href="/wiki/Jenny_Lind" title="Jenny Lind">Jenny Lind</a> spent the last years of her life living at Wynd\'s Point near the British Camp. She is buried in Great Malvern cemetery.<sup id="cite_ref-lindbeacon_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lindbeacon-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">Literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Malvern_Hills&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The Malvern Hills were the inspiration and setting for the famous 14th-century poem <i>The Visions of <a href="/wiki/Piers_Plowman" title="Piers Plowman">Piers Plowman</a></i> (1362) by <a href="/wiki/William_Langland" title="William Langland">William Langland</a>, who was possibly educated at the priory of Great Malvern.<sup id="cite_ref-Burrow1996_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Burrow1996-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> The earliest poetical allusion to the Malvern Hills occurs in the poem: "<i>And on a Maye mornynge on Malverne hylles</i>".<sup id="cite_ref-skeat1886_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-skeat1886-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-VisionofPiercePlowman1550_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VisionofPiercePlowman1550-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The poet <a href="/wiki/W._H._Auden" title="W. H. Auden">W. H. Auden</a> taught for three years at <a href="/wiki/The_Downs_School_(Herefordshire)" class="mw-redirect" title="The Downs School (Herefordshire)">The Downs School</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colwall" title="Colwall">Colwall</a>, in the Malvern Hills. He spent three years at the school in the 1930s and wrote some of his finest early love poems there, including: "This Lunar Beauty"; "Let Your Sleeping Head"; "My Love, Fish in the Unruffled Lakes"; and "Out on the Lawn I Lie in Bed". He also wrote a long poem about the hills and their views, called simply <i>The Malverns</i>.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/J.R.R._Tolkien" class="mw-redirect" title="J.R.R. Tolkien">J.R.R. Tolkien</a> found inspiration in the Malvern landscape<sup id="cite_ref-Duriez1992_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Duriez1992-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> which he had viewed from his childhood home in <a href="/wiki/Birmingham" title="Birmingham">Birmingham</a> and his brother Hilary\'s home near <a href="/wiki/Evesham" title="Evesham">Evesham</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-sayer-tolkien_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sayer-tolkien-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> He was introduced to the area by <a href="/wiki/C._S._Lewis" title="C. S. Lewis">C. S. Lewis</a>, who had brought him here to meet <a href="/wiki/George_Sayer" title="George Sayer">George Sayer</a>, the Head of English at <a href="/wiki/Malvern_College" title="Malvern College">Malvern College</a>. Sayer had been a student of Lewis, and became his biographer, and together with them Tolkien would walk the Malvern Hills. Recordings of Tolkien reading excerpts from <i><a href="/wiki/The_Hobbit" title="The Hobbit">The Hobbit</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lord_of_the_Rings" title="The Lord of the Rings">The Lord of the Rings</a></i> were made in Malvern in 1952, at the home of George Sayer. The recordings were later issued on long-playing <a href="/wiki/Gramophone_record" class="mw-redirect" title="Gramophone record">gramophone records</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Humphrey77_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Humphrey77-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> In the liner notes for <i>J.R.R. Tolkien Reads and Sings his The Hobbit &amp; The Fellowship of the Ring</i>, George Sayer wrote that Tolkien would relive the book as they walked and compared parts of the Malvern Hills to the <a href="/wiki/White_Mountains_(Middle-earth)" title="White Mountains (Middle-earth)">White Mountains of Gondor</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-sayer-tolkien_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sayer-tolkien-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The poet <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Barrett_Browning" title="Elizabeth Barrett Browning">Elizabeth Barrett Browning</a> spent her childhood at <a href="/wiki/Hope_End" title="Hope End">Hope End</a>, a 500-acre (2.0&#160;km<sup>2</sup>) estate near the Malvern Hills in <a href="/wiki/Ledbury" title="Ledbury">Ledbury</a>, Herefordshire. Her time at Hope End would inspire her in later life to write <i><a href="/wiki/Aurora_Leigh" title="Aurora Leigh">Aurora Leigh</a></i>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>In <i>Early British Trackways</i> <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Watkins" title="Alfred Watkins">Alfred Watkins</a> theorised that a <a href="/wiki/Ley_line" title="Ley line">ley line</a> passed along the Malvern Hills through several wells including St Ann\'s Well, <a href="/wiki/Holy_Well,_Malvern" title="Holy Well, Malvern">Holy Well</a>, Walms Well and St. Pewtress Well. Interest in Watkin\'s theories subsided in the 1930s but saw a revival in the late 1960s.<sup id="cite_ref-Watkins1921_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watkins1921-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> In <i>The Ley Hunter\'s Companion</i> (1979) <a href="/wiki/Paul_Devereux" title="Paul Devereux">Paul Devereux</a> theorised that a 10-mile alignment he called the "Malvern Ley" passed through St Ann\'s Well, the <a href="/wiki/Wyche,_Worcestershire" title="Wyche, Worcestershire">Wyche Cutting</a>, a section of the <a href="/wiki/British_Camp#The_Shire_Ditch" title="British Camp">Shire Ditch</a>, Midsummer Hill, <a href="/wiki/Whiteleaved_Oak" title="Whiteleaved Oak">Whiteleaved Oak</a>, Redmarley D\'Abitot and Pauntley.<sup id="cite_ref-Devereaux1979_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Devereaux1979-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> In <i>City of Revelation</i> (1973) British author <a href="/wiki/John_Michell_(writer)" title="John Michell (writer)">John Michell</a> theorised that Whiteleaved Oak is the centre of a circular alignment he called the "Circle of Perpetual Choirs" and is equidistant from <a href="/wiki/Glastonbury" title="Glastonbury">Glastonbury</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stonehenge" title="Stonehenge">Stonehenge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Goring-on-Thames" title="Goring-on-Thames">Goring-on-Thames</a> and <a href="/wiki/Llantwit_Major" title="Llantwit Major">Llantwit Major</a>. The theory was investigated by the <a href="/wiki/British_Society_of_Dowsers" title="British Society of Dowsers">British Society of Dowsers</a> and used as background material by <a href="/wiki/Phil_Rickman" title="Phil Rickman">Phil Rickman</a> in his novel <i>The Remains of an Altar</i> (2006).<sup id="cite_ref-Michell1973_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Michell1973-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rickman2006_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rickman2006-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>\n"Malvern Hills" is the third short story in Japanese-English author <a href="/wiki/Kazuo_Ishiguro" title="Kazuo Ishiguro">Kazuo Ishiguro</a>\'s collection <i><a href="/wiki/Nocturnes_(Ishiguro_book)" class="mw-redirect" title="Nocturnes (Ishiguro book)">Nocturnes</a></i> (2009).\n</p><p>The legend of the Shadow of the Ragged Stone, a shadow appearing to arise from the hilltop under particular meteorological conditions said to bring ill-fortune to those on whom it falls, features in many literary sources. It is the subject of the novel "The Shadow of the Ragged Stone" by Charles F. Grindrod, which tells the story of a monk who in punishment for breaking his vow of chastity was compelled to crawl up the hill every day on his hands and knees, and died cursing the hill and all on whom its shadow should fall.<sup id="cite_ref-raggedshadow_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-raggedshadow-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Art">Art</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Malvern_Hills&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Art">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Paintings of the Malvern Hills include <a href="/wiki/Henry_Harris_Lines" title="Henry Harris Lines">Henry Harris Lines</a>\'s <i>The British Camp and Herefordshire Beacon</i> (1872), now in the Worcester City Museums.<sup id="cite_ref-HenryHarrisLines1872_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HenryHarrisLines1872-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/David_Prentice" title="David Prentice">David Prentice</a>, founder member of <a href="/wiki/Birmingham" title="Birmingham">Birmingham</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Ikon_Gallery" title="Ikon Gallery">Ikon Gallery</a>, has been painting the Malvern Hills since 1986.<sup id="cite_ref-IkonGallery_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IkonGallery-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Prentice2011_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prentice2011-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Paul_Nash_(artist)" title="Paul Nash (artist)">Paul Nash</a> made paintings of the hills from \'Madams\' in Gloucestershire<sup id="cite_ref-AndersonMeyrickNahum2008_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AndersonMeyrickNahum2008-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> and from the \'Rising Sun\' hotel on <a href="/wiki/Cleeve_Hill" title="Cleeve Hill">Cleeve Hill</a> near <a href="/wiki/Cheltenham" title="Cheltenham">Cheltenham</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Eates1973_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eates1973-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Dame_Laura_Knight" class="mw-redirect" title="Dame Laura Knight">Dame Laura Knight</a> painted in a studio near Wynds Point below British Camp.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Television">Television</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Malvern_Hills&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Television">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The opening scene in <i><a href="/wiki/Elgar_(film)" title="Elgar (film)">Elgar</a></i>, a drama documentary made in 1962 by the British director <a href="/wiki/Ken_Russell" title="Ken Russell">Ken Russell</a>, depicts a young Elgar riding a white pony over the Malvern Hills.<sup id="cite_ref-TheMusicalLegacyOfKenRussell_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TheMusicalLegacyOfKenRussell-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Made for BBC Television\'s long-running <i><a href="/wiki/Monitor_(UK_TV_series)" class="mw-redirect" title="Monitor (UK TV series)">Monitor</a></i> programme, it dramatised the life of the composer Edward Elgar. The film significantly raised the public profile of the composer.<sup id="cite_ref-Elgar1962_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Elgar1962-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Tank Quarry on <a href="/wiki/North_Hill,_Malvern" title="North Hill, Malvern">North Hill</a> and West of England Quarry on the <a href="/wiki/Worcestershire_Beacon" title="Worcestershire Beacon">Worcestershire Beacon</a> were used as locations in the <i><a href="/wiki/Doctor_Who" title="Doctor Who">Doctor Who</a></i> serial <i><a href="/wiki/The_Krotons" title="The Krotons">The Krotons</a></i>, starring <a href="/wiki/Patrick_Troughton" title="Patrick Troughton">Patrick Troughton</a>. The serial was broadcast in four weekly parts from 28 December 1968 to 18 January 1969.<sup id="cite_ref-DrWhoTankQuarry_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DrWhoTankQuarry-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-DrWhoWestofEnglandQuarry_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DrWhoWestofEnglandQuarry-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BBCWorlwideTV_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBCWorlwideTV-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Malvern Hills are the backdrop for <a href="/wiki/Penda%27s_Fen" title="Penda&#39;s Fen">Penda\'s Fen</a>, a 1974 British television play written by <a href="/wiki/David_Rudkin" title="David Rudkin">David Rudkin</a> and directed by <a href="/wiki/Alan_Clarke" title="Alan Clarke">Alan Clarke</a> for the BBC\'s <a href="/wiki/Play_for_Today" title="Play for Today">Play for Today</a> series.  It tells the story of Stephen, a pastor\'s son who has <a href="/wiki/Vision_(spirituality)" title="Vision (spirituality)">visions</a> of angels, Edward Elgar, and <a href="/wiki/King_Penda" class="mw-redirect" title="King Penda">King Penda</a>, the last <a href="/wiki/Pagan" class="mw-redirect" title="Pagan">pagan</a> ruler of England.<sup id="cite_ref-Rolinson2005_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rolinson2005-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>\n<sup id="cite_ref-PendasFen_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PendasFen-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> The final scene of the play, where the protagonist has an <a href="/wiki/Apparitional_experience" title="Apparitional experience">apparitional experience</a> of King Penda and the "mother and father of England", is set on the Malvern Hills.<sup id="cite_ref-Rudkin1998_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rudkin1998-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Hills">The Hills</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Malvern_Hills&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: The Hills">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Malvern_Hills_Location.PNG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Malvern_Hills_Location.PNG/220px-Malvern_Hills_Location.PNG" width="220" height="282" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Malvern_Hills_Location.PNG/330px-Malvern_Hills_Location.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e2/Malvern_Hills_Location.PNG 2x" data-file-width="423" data-file-height="542" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Malvern_Hills_Location.PNG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Location within England</div></div></div>\n<p>A list of the hills in their order from north to south is shown below:<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJJ_of_the_Cooper_Partnership20096_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJJ_of_the_Cooper_Partnership20096-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable sortable">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Hill\n</th>\n<th>Elevation (ft)\n</th>\n<th>Elevation (m)\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/End_Hill" title="End Hill">End Hill</a></td>\n<td>1,079</td>\n<td>329\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Table_Hill" title="Table Hill">Table Hill</a></td>\n<td>1,224</td>\n<td>373\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/North_Hill,_Malvern" title="North Hill, Malvern">North Hill</a></td>\n<td>1,303</td>\n<td>397\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Sugarloaf_Hill,_Malvern" title="Sugarloaf Hill, Malvern">Sugarloaf Hill</a></td>\n<td>1,207</td>\n<td>368\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Worcestershire_Beacon" title="Worcestershire Beacon">Worcestershire Beacon</a></td>\n<td>1,395</td>\n<td>425\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Summer Hill</td>\n<td>1,253</td>\n<td>382\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Perseverance Hill</td>\n<td>1,066</td>\n<td>325\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Jubilee_Hill" title="Jubilee Hill">Jubilee Hill</a></td>\n<td>1,073</td>\n<td>327\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a (east top)</td>\n<td>820</td>\n<td>250\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Raggedstone_Hill" title="Raggedstone Hill">Raggedstone Hill</a> (west href="/wiki/M5_motorway" title="M5 motorway">M5 motorway</a>, particularly between Junction 7 at <a href="/wiki/Worcester" title="Worcester">Worcester</a> (south) and Junction 9 at <a href="/wiki/Tewkesbury" title="Tewkesbury">Tewkesbury</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right:auto; overflow:hidden; width:auto; max-width:808px;">\n<div class="thumbinner"><div class="noresize overflowbugx" style="overflow:auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Little_Malvern_Hills_panorama.jpg" class="image" title="Panorama of the Malvern Hills (AONB) with Little Malvern Priory taking centre stage."><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Little_Malvern_Hills_panorama.jpg/800px-Little_Malvern_Hills_panorama.jpg" width="800" class="thumbcaption" style="text-align:center"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Little_Malvern_Hills_panorama.jpg" title="File:Little Malvern Hills panorama.jpg"> </a></div>Panorama of the Malvern Hills (AONB) with Little Malvern Priory taking centre stage.</div>\n</div></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Vistas">Vistas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Malvern_Hills&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Vistas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Between 1999 and 2000, the Heart of England Tourist Board carried out a survey of visitors to the Malvern hills on behalf of the Malvern Hills Area of Outstanding Natural Beauty Partnership (AONB). Those questioned indicated that the thing they liked most about the hills was "the scenery and views".<sup id="cite_ref-Views-page-4_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Views-page-4-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>[The Malvern Hills form] an island of high ground surrounded by lower lying land, most noticeably to the east. As a result, the [h]ills are clearly visible and easily recognisable from a considerable distance away [and] constitute an iconic feature in the local and regional landscape.<sup id="cite_ref-Views-page-4_102-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Views-page-4-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>The AONB commissioned study to "identify and assess a selection of key views to and from the Malvern Hills" it was carried out in 2009 by Cooper Partnership Ltd, a firm of Chartered Landscape Architects.<sup id="cite_ref-Views-page-4_102-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Views-page-4-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-JJ_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JJ-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>\nThis information was gathered not only so that the best vistas could be made known to a wider public, but also as an intelligence gathering so that proposed changes to the landscape both in and outside the Malvern Hills area of outstanding beauty (such as the building of wind turbines) could be assessed against the impact those developments would have on the Malvern Hills area of outstanding beauty.<sup id="cite_ref-Views-page-4_102-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Views-page-4-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>\nThe Cooper Partnership identified 50 key views from vantage points on the Malvern hills,<sup id="cite_ref-Views-page-4_102-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Views-page-4-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJJ_of_the_Cooper_Partnership200919\xe2\x80\x9326_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJJ_of_the_Cooper_Partnership200919\xe2\x80\x9326-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> and from the surrounding area. These were:<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJJ_of_the_Cooper_Partnership200919\xe2\x80\x9335_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJJ_of_the_Cooper_Partnership200919\xe2\x80\x9335-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable sortable">\n<caption>Vistas within the AONB
